  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer3984923984864172756">Weedon Grossmith's 1892 book presents the details of English suburban life through the anxious and accident-prone character of Charles Pooter. Pooter's diary chronicles his daily routine, which includes small parties, minor embarrassments, home improvements, and his relationship with a troublesome son. The small minded but essentially decent suburban world he inhabits is b</span>
  <span id="freeText3984923984864172756" style="display:none">Weedon Grossmith's 1892 book presents the details of English suburban life through the anxious and accident-prone character of Charles Pooter. Pooter's diary chronicles his daily routine, which includes small parties, minor embarrassments, home improvements, and his relationship with a troublesome son. The small minded but essentially decent suburban world he inhabits is both hilarious and painfully familiar. This edition features Weedon Grossmith's illustrations and an introduction which discusses the story's social context.</span>

<div id="review_2085254437" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2085254437" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2085254437?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=nice-try-but-no-cigars">nice-try-but-no-cigars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2085254437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer292441264981408339">Well, what can I say? <br/><br/>Bloggers, Facebookers - who would have thought you had a predecessor in Victorian England?<br/> <br/>Who would have thought the vain thoughts and actions of a completely unimportant person with big ideas about his own personality were meticulously documented and published back then already, including lists of food, what to wear on what occasion, social encounters, small run-ins with friends and family, hopelessly disappointing egocentric grown-up children? If he had had a smartphone,</span>
  <span id="freeText292441264981408339" style="display:none">Well, what can I say? <br /><br />Bloggers, Facebookers - who would have thought you had a predecessor in Victorian England?<br /> <br />Who would have thought the vain thoughts and actions of a completely unimportant person with big ideas about his own personality were meticulously documented and published back then already, including lists of food, what to wear on what occasion, social encounters, small run-ins with friends and family, hopelessly disappointing egocentric grown-up children? If he had had a smartphone, the dear Mr Nobody would have posted a picture of his dinner on his blog each night. As it is, he simply writes down the minute by minute of his event-less life. He is so proud of his puns that he repeats them to himself and laughs out loud. If he had a blog, he would count the likes and share "the joke of the day" with all his acquaintances. <br /><br />An unspectacular read, which leaves two reflections. First of all, humans have always had the need to be "seen" and "heard" by others, to distinguish themselves from the crowd and to stick out. That is not new, and our technology simply makes it easier to reach outside our own community. My second thought was that the novel obviously is sarcastic, making fun of this need. It seems to me that it is harder to laugh at it nowadays, as we all indulge in the illusion of visibility (to different degrees) today. Who can still laugh at it silently? Without repeating the joke on Goodreads, counting the likes? <br /><br />Not me. For here I am, writing another review to be posted and shared. As much as I shun other social media, Goodreads satisfies that wish to share in my world, and I would be beyond hypocritical if I made fun of the human need of the boring, boasting Everyman in the diary. He might be a Nobody, but he surely pointed towards the future in a more realistic way than many other Victorian heroes.<br /><br />Not sure if I recommend the novel, as it is rather boring, like reading online what a friend had for breakfast, with an accompanying picture, but on the other hand, we like that kind of sharing, don't we?</span>
  <a data-text-id="292441264981408339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_310379289" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310379289" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310379289?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199540150" href="/book/show/3722025-the-diary-of-a-nobody">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pre-1900s">pre-1900s</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310379289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13174793961367571740"><b>11 April</b><br/><br/>Sat down to write a capsule review of <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i>. Interrupted by a loving thump at the door. It was Mark Nicholls from my review of Italo Calvino’s <i>If on a Winter’s Night a Traveller</i>, a piece of spoof metafiction that ranks as my most liked GR review. I studied my 23-year-old self carefully then looked at my 25-year-old self and noted nothing had changed facially in two years except I was even more handsomely bespectacled. “Would you like to buy a copy of . . . ?” he began, bu</span>
  <span id="freeText13174793961367571740" style="display:none"><b>11 April</b><br /><br />Sat down to write a capsule review of <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i>. Interrupted by a loving thump at the door. It was Mark Nicholls from my review of Italo Calvino’s <i>If on a Winter’s Night a Traveller</i>, a piece of spoof metafiction that ranks as my most liked GR review. I studied my 23-year-old self carefully then looked at my 25-year-old self and noted nothing had changed facially in two years except I was even more handsomely bespectacled. “Would you like to buy a copy of . . . ?” he began, but I’d heard it before. After all, I wrote it. “Finished that novel we started in 2009 yet?” he asked snidely. “Yes! I finished that like a month ago,” I said, triumphantly. Mark Nicholls from 2009 circled the Mark Nicholls from 2012 like a toreador taunting a pacifist bull. “Wow. Speedy Gonzalez. You must be the new Joyce Carol Oates,” he said. I snickered, neglecting to tell him about our vagina transplant.<br /><br /><b>12 April</b><br /><br />I change to the present tense since the review is being written today, contrary to the opening sentence. That’s an example of what we call in the trade “unreliable narration.” Having doubts about writing a spoof diary review, despite having spoofed since my teens. I put on the new Big Sexy Noise album, <i>Trust the Witch</i>. Lydia Lunch appears on my desk and berates me for being a pussywhipped pastyasted whitebred chickenshed motherloving dolescrouging booksucking bitchboy. I tell her that’s far too many dashless hybrid words for a Thursday. She laughs and we have anal and a slice of malt loaf.<br /><br /><b>13 April</b><br /><br />I will change tense, since this day follows the day on which the review was written. The question will arise, however, as to whether the first sentence needed a tense change, seeing it was written yesterday. (Although this isn’t true either—the review was actually written on the Wednesday night with a view to being posted on the Thursday!) I will walk to cupboard, where Dostoevsky’s skin is hanging on a coat hanger, awaiting its body. The doorbell will ring. A fleshy bone arrangement with organs will stand there and say: “Looking for Fyodor’s skin. Is he in?” I will wrinkle my beautiful eyes. “How do you know your skin’s a she?” I will ask. “All women will be brought low beneath the eyes of our Creator!” he will shout. “OK, cool it, come in,” I’ll say. “Ooh, using contractions now, are we?” he’ll ask. I’ll say: “Yup.”<br /><br /><b>10 April</b><br /><br />I started to read <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i>. I thought how clever it might be to write a spoof review, using surreal antics as a contrast to the novel’s straight-laced satire. I realised that would probably be a mistake. <br /><br /><b>25 April (morning)</b><br /><br />Manny turns up fourteen days too late for the review. He tries to attract attention by pirouetting on the coffee table, but at his age the best he can manage is a forward roll on the settee. Winnie the Pooh walks in and bitches about his boyfriend Dante, who won’t go all the way with him. “To Hell and back, he says!” Pooh sneers. “I’m not that kind of bear.” Manny’s had enough with this review and returns to <i>Yoga For Men</i> where he demonstrates a perfect backwards lotuscrab triple-swivel manoeuvre while sucking a toffee apple.<br /><br /><b>25 April (afternoon)</b><br /><br />Then Knig-o-lass walks in and says: “This suspirro is a cataclysmic feuerwerk of inconsequentialityness.” She curtsies to Pooh and then scarpers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13174793961367571740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12033988124817871353">The diary of my everyday life would be very boring, and by most measure so is Charles Pooter's. Living in late Victorian Era England, Pooter and his wife Carrie are stuck deep into middle class society. But Pooter knows his place, and he seems quite happy to make the best of it. He pays homage to his employer, appreciates his modest home, and is satisfied with his occasional chance to rub shoulders with the upper class at the Lord Mayors Ball. He daily frets over things like shirt collars, boot </span>
  <span id="freeText12033988124817871353" style="display:none">The diary of my everyday life would be very boring, and by most measure so is Charles Pooter's. Living in late Victorian Era England, Pooter and his wife Carrie are stuck deep into middle class society. But Pooter knows his place, and he seems quite happy to make the best of it. He pays homage to his employer, appreciates his modest home, and is satisfied with his occasional chance to rub shoulders with the upper class at the Lord Mayors Ball. He daily frets over things like shirt collars, boot black, his housekeepers shortcomings, and so on. But his life, and the diary's, is helped along by his two neighbors and friends, Gowing and Cummings, who are always popping in, always going and coming, and if you get that little joke, then you will get the essence of this story and the essence of Charles Pooter. Gowing and Cummings are to Charles Pooter what Fred and Ethyl Mertz were to Lucy and Desi. I enjoyed the deadpan humor, and my enjoyment was enhanced by the splendid narration of the book by Martin Clifton.</span>

<div id="review_493764984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/493764984" />
    <a title="Jonathan Stephenson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11886042-jonathan-stephenson"><img alt="Jonathan Stephenson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351853680p2/11886042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/493764984?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Stephenson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Stephenson" href="/user/show/11886042-jonathan-stephenson">Jonathan Stephenson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781853262012" href="/book/show/880611.The_Diary_of_a_Nobody">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer493764984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14436663052211395101">Brilliant! A book filled with unimportant characters, not about anything in particular, in which nothing much happens. Well not exactly, this is a satire on being ordinary.<br/><br/>Admittedly modern readers may not find it as funny as when it was first published in <i>Punch</i> in the late 19th century, as the context and detail of Victorian middle-class values that it parodies are no longer an immediately understood reference point and tastes in, as well as expectations of, humour have moved on. What it pokes </span>
  <span id="freeText14436663052211395101" style="display:none">Brilliant! A book filled with unimportant characters, not about anything in particular, in which nothing much happens. Well not exactly, this is a satire on being ordinary.<br /><br />Admittedly modern readers may not find it as funny as when it was first published in <i>Punch</i> in the late 19th century, as the context and detail of Victorian middle-class values that it parodies are no longer an immediately understood reference point and tastes in, as well as expectations of, humour have moved on. What it pokes fun at still exists in present day society and everyday life though and the subtle, not so subtle, witty and cringe-making elements of its sending up have resurfaced many times over in other comic guises and genre.<br /><br />Expect a gentle sit-com, not laugh out loud stand-up style—and tune in to the delicate ridiculing of social attitudes and individual stereotypes on offer. It is full of satirical one liners and awkward moments that still have contemporary resonance.<br /><br /><i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> is exactly what its title says it is. There is no complex comic plot or hilarious dénouement. The humour is definitely quite ‘English’ and its target is urban life. If you get that it is still relevant and very amusing to read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14436663052211395101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_195196363" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195196363" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it



          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">the light hearted</span></div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer195196363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5044384272677560716">This reminded me of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_' title='Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome' rel='nofollow noopener'>Three Men in a Boat</a> in that I don't feel that some great moments add up to a great book.<br/><br/>A diary format allowed the Grossmiths to have a series of comic <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ I use the term lightly - comic at least in their opinion, the reader will make up their own mind  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> incidents without the inconvenience of a plot, although there are some long running story lines that are tied up by the end of the book.<br/> <br/>The diary is written by Mr Pooter, a senior bank clerk who wo</span>
  <span id="freeText5044384272677560716" style="display:none">This reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_" title="Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a> in that I don't feel that some great moments add up to a great book.<br /><br />A diary format allowed the Grossmiths to have a series of comic <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I use the term lightly - comic at least in their opinion, the reader will make up their own mind  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> incidents without the inconvenience of a plot, although there are some long running story lines that are tied up by the end of the book.<br /> <br />The diary is written by Mr Pooter, a senior bank clerk who works in the City of London <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ now that's a Britishism...For none UKers the City of London is a separate legal entity located in central London where various financial institutions have their headquarters <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and records the trials and tribulations of his late Victorian life after moving into a new home.<br /><br />The idea is that you find his self-importance and occasional pomposities amusing, and it helps to be socially superior to characters of this sort for the book to work. In the humour of an in-egalitarian society, jokes turn on the snobbishness and angst of middle-class Victorian life, for example Pooter is extremely satisfied to be invited to a fancy event and extremely dissatisfied when he finds one of his neighbours - who in his eyes is only a tradesman - is also there. I'm of too low a social <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=K2k1iRD2f-c" rel="nofollow noopener">class</a> to be as thoroughly amused as the target audience <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ or perhaps my funny bone simply has a different angle, you've got to be open minded about these things <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, for instance I found Pooter's pride and satisfaction in the idea of having his son work in the bank alongside him, the two of them taking the omnibus into the city together, tender rather than comic. <br /> <br />The character names like Pooter or his friends Cummings and Gowings give you another idea of the Grossmith's sense of humour. If you're not smiling or groaning at those then your smile to page ratio is lightly to be low. <br /><br />However there are some ideas that build up into great comic scenes, notably those involving a tin of red paint and what to do with it <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[answer: paint many, many things<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[just possibly including some things best not painted  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />If you are going to read it, I recommend an edition with the original illustrations.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23326236" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5629441458667242182">Overrated fictional "diary" of a middle-aged, middle-class, angst-ridden, Victorian middle-manager, dealing with inconsequential daily irritations in a dry (but not especially funny) way. <br/><br/>The authors (brothers, George and Weedon, despite GR listing just one) also like self-conscious puns. I like puns, but I like a degree of subtlety that's lacking. For example, a couple of friends have punning names, but lest the reader fail to notice, out comes the heavy hammer:<br/>"<i>Doesn't it seem odd that Gowing'</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5629441458667242182" style="display:none">Overrated fictional "diary" of a middle-aged, middle-class, angst-ridden, Victorian middle-manager, dealing with inconsequential daily irritations in a dry (but not especially funny) way. <br /><br />The authors (brothers, George and Weedon, despite GR listing just one) also like self-conscious puns. I like puns, but I like a degree of subtlety that's lacking. For example, a couple of friends have punning names, but lest the reader fail to notice, out comes the heavy hammer:<br />"<i>Doesn't it seem odd that Gowing's always coming and Cummings is always going?</i>"<br /><br />One measure of great art (and I count literature as art) is that you get something different from it each time. Books like this challenge that view: it's loved and lauded by many, and I remember enjoying it in my late teens or early 20s, but a decade or two later, it was very different. I'm pretty sure that doesn't make it great, though! It also demonstrates the risk of revisiting things one has warm but distant memories of. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5629441458667242182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_557253439" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/557253439" />
    <a title="Grace Harwood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16438199-grace-harwood"><img alt="Grace Harwood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658079p2/16438199.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/557253439?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Grace Harwood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Grace Harwood" href="/user/show/16438199-grace-harwood">Grace Harwood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer557253439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13731960522009248854">"Why should I not publish my diary? I have often seen reminiscences of people I have never even heard of, and I fail to see - because I do not happen to be a 'Somebody' - why my diary should not be interesting."<br/><br/>And thank goodness that Charles Pooter, ordinary clerk and Victorian family man decided to follow this course. The humour is gentle but had me in stitches at times and is still as funny today as it must have been for its contemporary audience of Punch readers in the 1890s. One is torn bet</span>
  <span id="freeText13731960522009248854" style="display:none">"Why should I not publish my diary? I have often seen reminiscences of people I have never even heard of, and I fail to see - because I do not happen to be a 'Somebody' - why my diary should not be interesting."<br /><br />And thank goodness that Charles Pooter, ordinary clerk and Victorian family man decided to follow this course. The humour is gentle but had me in stitches at times and is still as funny today as it must have been for its contemporary audience of Punch readers in the 1890s. One is torn between feeling desperately sorry for Mr Pooter (and recognising that we can all be impossibly pompous and self-important at times) and laughing out loud at the many slights and injuries he receives at the hands of tradesmen and his fellow clerks. When William (now self-styling himself by his middle name "Lupin") arrives on the scene, the action picks up and the scene is set for much more hilarity as Lupin disregards his father and his old-fashioned ways and sets off on a new-fangled modern course which Pooter can only believe will lead to disgrace. <br /><br />In some ways this is a comedy, but it is also a window into Victorian society from the point of view of the middle-class working man and his family. We have all the fads of the Victorian age (spiritualism makes an interesting appearance with even the sceptical Pooter getting caught up in the seances his wife and her friend start hosting) and a representation of the conflict between the thirst for progress and the concern inherent within this about "dangerous" ideas which epitomises this age. Pooter is half-pleased, half-concerned when he notes that his son Lupin has some of the same traits as the progressive Huttle: "I feel proud to think Lupin does resemble Mr. Huttle in some ways. Lupin, like Mr Huttle, has original and sometimes wonderful ideas; but it is those ideas that are so dangerous. They make men extremely rich or extremely poor. They make or break men. I always feel people are happier who live a simple unsophisticated life. I believe I am happy because I am not ambitious."<br /><br />This is a lovely book - the office scenes read a bit like "The Victorian Office" with Pitt the waggish junior clerk playing the part of Tim Canterbury to Pooter's David Brent. I so enjoyed reading it and it was much, much too short for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13731960522009248854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25099685?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/1134884-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1134884-scott?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1134884-scott?shelf=humor">humor</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25099685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12039026680484462754">Bumbling Charles Pooter's memoir of timeless suburban angst <i> The Diary of a Nobody</i> (1892) remains remarkably modern and amusing even a century after it was first printed in <i>Punch</i>. Pooter can't understand his son's slang or dismal work ethic ... his wife is spellbound by new age spiritualism ... his friends continually clean out his larder and drain his wine bottles without reciprocating ... the plumbing doesn't work ... the neighbors throw garbage into his garden ... and fate seems determined to</span>
  <span id="freeText12039026680484462754" style="display:none">Bumbling Charles Pooter's memoir of timeless suburban angst <i> The Diary of a Nobody</i> (1892) remains remarkably modern and amusing even a century after it was first printed in <i>Punch</i>. Pooter can't understand his son's slang or dismal work ethic ... his wife is spellbound by new age spiritualism ... his friends continually clean out his larder and drain his wine bottles without reciprocating ... the plumbing doesn't work ... the neighbors throw garbage into his garden ... and fate seems determined to insult and embarrass the stodgy old Pooter at every turn. And yet you can't help but like him as he strives to retain his dignity, grace, and composure in the face of life's small yet annoying trials. Best read a few pages at a time, the <i>Diary</i> offers wonderful comic relief when read along with heavier contemporary novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12039026680484462754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1524223696" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1524223696?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/11351200-leslie">Leslie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10387039-the-diary-of-a-nobody">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1524223696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4241515105368874465">Mr. Pooter decides to keep a diary in the hopes of one day becoming the Pepys of the late Victorian era. He is a clerk of a somewhat stuffy and pompous nature but with a love of bad puns and jokes (luckily for him his wife shares his sense of humor!). <br/><br/>I found him a little reminiscent of "The Irish R.M." in his never-ending series of domestic mishaps - both of these books amuse yet puzzle me. As a person who has never even seen a domestic servant much less employed one, the battle of control betw</span>
  <span id="freeText4241515105368874465" style="display:none">Mr. Pooter decides to keep a diary in the hopes of one day becoming the Pepys of the late Victorian era. He is a clerk of a somewhat stuffy and pompous nature but with a love of bad puns and jokes (luckily for him his wife shares his sense of humor!). <br /><br />I found him a little reminiscent of "The Irish R.M." in his never-ending series of domestic mishaps - both of these books amuse yet puzzle me. As a person who has never even seen a domestic servant much less employed one, the battle of control between master &amp; servant baffles me to some extent. It clearly baffles Mr. Pooter as well! He persists in thinking that he is the master and so is deserving of respect despite the fact that he rarely gets that respect even from his own son. <br /><br />Grossmith's satire has captured the beginning of the end for the middle-class Victorian way of life with Pooter and his son. Pooter's worries about his son Lupin's future could be seen as a reflection of a greater concern about security and expectations for the middle-class workers and their families if the rigidity of the old-fashioned methods gives way, while Lupin's attitudes point up the impatience of the rising generation with the adherence to outmoded ideas and practices.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4241515105368874465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_310402489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310402489" />
    <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi"><img alt="Lorenzo Berardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417557095p2/183865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310402489?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorenzo Berardi" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi">Lorenzo Berardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781853262012" href="/book/show/880611.The_Diary_of_a_Nobody">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=a-my-english-library">a-my-english-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310402489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3713887465053892901">It is with the uttermost pleasure that I read through the diary of Mr Charles Pooter of Holloway, London. <br/>Mark my words, this gentleman was certainly not a Nobody. <br/> <br/>I am aware that the excellent Mrs Pooter and the author's own son, Mr Lupin Pooter, didn't value the diary much. Nonetheless, it is my strong belief that they are both mistaken in this respect. <br/><br/>By Jove! This distinguished gentleman - which is to say Mr Charles Pooter - not only mastered his business in the City but knew very well how</span>
  <span id="freeText3713887465053892901" style="display:none">It is with the uttermost pleasure that I read through the diary of Mr Charles Pooter of Holloway, London. <br />Mark my words, this gentleman was certainly not a Nobody. <br /> <br />I am aware that the excellent Mrs Pooter and the author's own son, Mr Lupin Pooter, didn't value the diary much. Nonetheless, it is my strong belief that they are both mistaken in this respect. <br /><br />By Jove! This distinguished gentleman - which is to say Mr Charles Pooter - not only mastered his business in the City but knew very well how to draw the most exquisite portrait of a suburban life. A life that you might think of as quintessentially dull, but it shines as actually quite amusing, refreshing and dignified in this most valued diary.<br />It is suffice to say that I am utterly delighted to discover that a man of such moral stature and of supremely noble behaviour such as Mr Pooter left his mark in the history of British literature. <br /><br />To tell you the truth, I value Mr Charles Pooter over the whole lot of the most accomplished humourists that England has had the privilege to breed. You might object that Jerome and Wodehouse did rather well in the same literary department and that this Nobody does not deserve to share their fame.<br />To which objection I reply in this way; true, good old John Klapka Jerome and P.G. Wodehouse might have been particularly good when it came to depict funny vignettes and unforgettable characters, but what Charles Pooter gave to the Anglo-Saxon readers is much more: and it is style.<br />In fact, I am proud to state that all of its undeniable mastery the work of both authors pale by comparison to the one of Mr Pooter of Holloway. <br /><br />By the by, shall we forget to mention Mr Pooter's uproarius word jokes? <br />No, we shall better not.<br />For Mr Charles Pooter was first and foremost a diarist and a chronicler of his times (in this respect quite capable to look at a master like Samuel Pepys eye to eye), but also one of the wittiest men around.<br /><br />How regretful to think that a gentleman of such intellectual stature didn't have the chance to meet with his peers!<br />For even though this diary covers a span of only a few months in the life and opinions of Charles Pooter, it is quite clear that his sharp wit was not recognized by his family, friends and acquaintances. <br /><br />And this Pooterish knowledge is excruciatingly painful to bear. <br />Hail to the brilliant Mr George Grossmith for making Mr Charles Pooter's literary legacy known to the readers of today! </span>
  <a data-text-id="3713887465053892901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3308529214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7855532147129153105">Every nobody is actually a somebody<br/><br/>Forgetting quarantines, Trump, lack of Chinese food, leaf blowers on Saturday mornings, raccoons in the trash, anthropology, and rainy Mondays, I took up THE DIARY OF A NOBODY, which I’d bought seven years ago at a local yard sale and completely neglected since then. Who had ever heard of this thin volume? Puttering about my attic bookshelves, I just needed a change of pace. And I definitely got it.<br/> <br/>This is a dry-humored comic masterpiece from late 19th century</span>
  <span id="freeText7855532147129153105" style="display:none">Every nobody is actually a somebody<br /><br />Forgetting quarantines, Trump, lack of Chinese food, leaf blowers on Saturday mornings, raccoons in the trash, anthropology, and rainy Mondays, I took up THE DIARY OF A NOBODY, which I’d bought seven years ago at a local yard sale and completely neglected since then. Who had ever heard of this thin volume? Puttering about my attic bookshelves, I just needed a change of pace. And I definitely got it.<br /> <br />This is a dry-humored comic masterpiece from late 19th century London. I chuckled my way through the whole thing and concluded that though technology has certainly changed, people haven’t. (An amazing observation. Nice work, Bob.) Charles Pooter works as a clerk, his wife is at home, and his son, Lupin, gets himself fired from his clerking job, chooses unsuitable friends and fiancées, yet seems to think he’s a lot smarter than Dad. All sorts of extremely mundane events and modest achievements or social faux pas take place. Sometimes Mr. Pooter realizes it, sometimes he doesn’t. The whole thing is written in an understated, blindly sincere tone. I loved it. His two friends are Cummings and Gowing. His boss is Mr. Perkupp. This dude paints his bathtub red, but it’s not waterproof paint. Pratfalls seem to be his bread and butter. If you don’t find yourself in here, then you ain’t lived. Or you never looked in the mirror and saw a Pooter. OK, maybe a Part-Time Pooter. <br /><br />My final words are: this is from the same country that produced Monty Python. They were Pooters on steroids. Check it out. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7855532147129153105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_596151146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/596151146" />
    <a title="Ivonne Rovira" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3803671-ivonne-rovira"><img alt="Ivonne Rovira" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339470651p2/3803671.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/596151146?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivonne Rovira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivonne Rovira" href="/user/show/3803671-ivonne-rovira">Ivonne Rovira</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6005134-the-diary-of-a-nobody">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer596151146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1079820084152231195">Thirty years before <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7330.Sinclair_Lewis' title='Sinclair Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sinclair Lewis</a> published <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169718.Babbit' title='Babbit by Sinclair Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Babbit</a> and set the standard for smug, self-important middle-class conformity, there was <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> and Charles Pooter. Pooter, a senior bank clerk in the City renting a home in the London suburb of Holloway, encapsulates Victorian respectability, snobbery, and pretensions. Pooter nearly invariably gets the short end of the stick in his interactions with his two neighbors, Cummings and Gowings; his spendthrift, reckless son Lupin; and the va</span>
  <span id="freeText1079820084152231195" style="display:none">Thirty years before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7330.Sinclair_Lewis" title="Sinclair Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">Sinclair Lewis</a> published <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169718.Babbit" title="Babbit by Sinclair Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">Babbit</a> and set the standard for smug, self-important middle-class conformity, there was <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> and Charles Pooter. Pooter, a senior bank clerk in the City renting a home in the London suburb of Holloway, encapsulates Victorian respectability, snobbery, and pretensions. Pooter nearly invariably gets the short end of the stick in his interactions with his two neighbors, Cummings and Gowings; his spendthrift, reckless son Lupin; and the various tradesmen and servants he attempts to bully. Slavishly devoted to his employer, Mr. Perkupp, Pooter tries without much luck to cut his son into the same mold. Instead, Lupin slacks at work and spends his nights engaged in amateur theatrics or carousing with his chums till all hours. What's a father to do? <br /><br />First serialized in <i>Punch</i> in 1888 and 1889, <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> was published in book form in 1892 and hasn't been out of print since. If you give this slim volume a chance (available for free in the Kindle format), you'll see why. Despite being a century old, <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> remains quite amusing and is laugh-out-loud funny in parts -- particularly in sections dealing with tradesmen or with Lupin's impetuous business dealings or love affairs. As long as there are self-satisfied <i>petit bourgeois</i> snobs, <i>The Diary of a Nobody</i> will continue to entertain.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1079820084152231195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1828645086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5305558843653729529">The perfect summer read. You can read it on the beach and expect to crack a few smiles or even, perhaps, a chuckle or two. <br/><br/>There's barely a story to this. A vaguely ridiculous middle class Victorian man decides to write a diary. His problems are mundane. Conflict with friends, troubles with his son, an obsession with promotion. I fear the humour is sometimes outdated, relying too much on repetition but most of the times it is genuinly funny. The ending is also clearly an attempt at coherency whi</span>
  <span id="freeText5305558843653729529" style="display:none">The perfect summer read. You can read it on the beach and expect to crack a few smiles or even, perhaps, a chuckle or two. <br /><br />There's barely a story to this. A vaguely ridiculous middle class Victorian man decides to write a diary. His problems are mundane. Conflict with friends, troubles with his son, an obsession with promotion. I fear the humour is sometimes outdated, relying too much on repetition but most of the times it is genuinly funny. The ending is also clearly an attempt at coherency which the story was never supposed to have. Still, a funny read, one I recommend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5305558843653729529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=authors-gh">authors-gh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=victorian-fiction">victorian-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99066609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11611249166295977701">I'd had this for a while and thought it would make good paired reading with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Penguin_Popular_Classics_' title='Three Men in a Boat (Penguin Popular Classics) by Jerome K. Jerome' rel='nofollow noopener'>Three Men on a Boat</a>, as they're both considered classics of British humor of about the same era. George Grossmith is perhaps best known as a long-time star of the D'Oyly Carte Opera Company, performing the comic baritone roles (Ko-Ko, Major-General Stanley, Sir Joseph Porter) in Gilbert and Sullivan's operas; his brother Weedon was largely an artist. <br/><br/>Their hero, Charles Pooter, is an ordinary middle-class clerk in London</span>
  <span id="freeText11611249166295977701" style="display:none">I'd had this for a while and thought it would make good paired reading with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Penguin_Popular_Classics_" title="Three Men in a Boat (Penguin Popular Classics) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men on a Boat</a>, as they're both considered classics of British humor of about the same era. George Grossmith is perhaps best known as a long-time star of the D'Oyly Carte Opera Company, performing the comic baritone roles (Ko-Ko, Major-General Stanley, Sir Joseph Porter) in Gilbert and Sullivan's operas; his brother Weedon was largely an artist. <br /><br />Their hero, Charles Pooter, is an ordinary middle-class clerk in London, who decides to keep and publish a diary, on the grounds that "I have often seen reminiscences of people I have never even heard of, and I fail to see -- because I do not happen to be a 'Somebody' -- why my diary should not be interesting." It is interesting, as a very funny, sometimes almost painful depiction of his small, suburban lifestyle, and although it's impossible not to laugh at Pooter, it's also impossible not to like him. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11611249166295977701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

I know I’m not supposed to, but shit puns make me happy…<br/><br/>‘The Diary of a Nobody’ goes nowhere and does nothing - but it is still an utterly compelling read. <br/><br/>It is from the novel’s central character that we derive the term ‘Pooterism’ (Noun: to take oneself grotesquely seriously.) But this is unfair. <br/><br/>Charles Pooter is constantly throwing out gags and half-arsed one-liners to people. It’s just unfortunate that only he (and occasionally his wife, Carrie) is bri</span>
  <span id="freeText14431224710291958364" style="display:none">I really like Pooter’s terrible jokes. I know I’m not supposed to, but shit puns make me happy…<br /><br />‘The Diary of a Nobody’ goes nowhere and does nothing - but it is still an utterly compelling read. <br /><br />It is from the novel’s central character that we derive the term ‘Pooterism’ (Noun: to take oneself grotesquely seriously.) But this is unfair. <br /><br />Charles Pooter is constantly throwing out gags and half-arsed one-liners to people. It’s just unfortunate that only he (and occasionally his wife, Carrie) is bright enough to understand them…<br /><br />Victorian society apparently looked quite darkly on terrible puns.*<br /><br />*Apart from when Dickens did them, obviously…</span>

<div id="review_1633654951" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1633654951" />
    <a title="Vimal Thiagarajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27250071-vimal-thiagarajan"><img alt="Vimal Thiagarajan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511490522p2/27250071.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1633654951?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vimal Thiagarajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vimal Thiagarajan" href="/user/show/27250071-vimal-thiagarajan">Vimal Thiagarajan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1633654951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4729190675209905176">A remarkably unique work of humour. Wasn't the Wodehousian kind with bubbling verve and<br/>contrivance nor the Jeromian kind with riotous and extreme slapsticks and engulfing philosophy. It sported laid-back and believable everyday humour with not so readily apparent existential undercurrents. Made for a very relaxing read, just what I've come to expect of a classic.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_558158460" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558158460" />
    <a title="Willis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5154645-willis"><img alt="Willis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311998062p2/5154645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558158460?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willis" href="/user/show/5154645-willis">Willis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558158460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7328321487099318872">Interesting read. It's not exciting or dazzling. It is "Seinfeld" in Victorian times. It is about nothing: just a man keeping a diary of his days. He and his wife live very ordinary, middle-class lives. He works for a financial firm. She keeps house. They live simply. Appearances are important to them, not only for his work but for their standing in the community. Their adult son moves back in with them (common in today's world), and his lifestyle, speech, and attitude is very different from his</span>
  <span id="freeText7328321487099318872" style="display:none">Interesting read. It's not exciting or dazzling. It is "Seinfeld" in Victorian times. It is about nothing: just a man keeping a diary of his days. He and his wife live very ordinary, middle-class lives. He works for a financial firm. She keeps house. They live simply. Appearances are important to them, not only for his work but for their standing in the community. Their adult son moves back in with them (common in today's world), and his lifestyle, speech, and attitude is very different from his parents (also very common). They have friends who pop in for visits without notice. They resent their friends' lack of manners, but still keep almost constant company with them. It is a big deal when they get invited to social events because it happens so infrequently. Everyone's feelings get hurt over minor jokes and misunderstandings, but they end up getting over the hurt and remain friends. Replace the Victorian settings with today's, and this could be my life or yours. This book celebrates the mundane rather than the extraordinary. It made me think about the parts of my life that are much as the Pooters' lives. Much easier and more pleasant to read than something by Dickens, and leaves out the mundane descriptions with which most writers of the Victorian age felt compelled to saturate their writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7328321487099318872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2063003307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2063003307" />
    <a title="Jaksen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen"><img alt="Jaksen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455934399p2/7233842.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2063003307?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaksen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaksen" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen">Jaksen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2063003307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17988747852966660418">I read this because Adam Dalgliesh - the MC in many of PD James' novels and short stories - gives his elderly aunt a copy for Christmas! I said to myself, never heard of that, is it a real book? It is.<br/><br/>It's funny in a sort of strained way, involving a middle-class gentleman, Mr. Charles Pooter, who keeps a diary for 18 months around the years 1892-1893. He quibbles and complains and goes through his daily routine - he's some sort of clerk at some sort of business requiring clerks - and finds humo</span>
  <span id="freeText17988747852966660418" style="display:none">I read this because Adam Dalgliesh - the MC in many of PD James' novels and short stories - gives his elderly aunt a copy for Christmas! I said to myself, never heard of that, is it a real book? It is.<br /><br />It's funny in a sort of strained way, involving a middle-class gentleman, Mr. Charles Pooter, who keeps a diary for 18 months around the years 1892-1893. He quibbles and complains and goes through his daily routine - he's some sort of clerk at some sort of business requiring clerks - and finds humor in puns. I mean, he roars at them, and often the others around him do as well. His wife is the English archetype, the long-suffering wife - but he adores her. His son, Lupin - who was born William, but prefers 'Lupin,' for reasons never explained - is a kind of 'ne'r do well,' always looking for a way to make a fast buck. (And omgoodness, the boy does use slang far too much! Always confusing his dear old dad! What are kids coming to these - those - days?) Mr. Pooter also has two friends, Cumming and Gowing, who are always...wait for it - did you see the pun there? <br /><br />The book, therefore, is a series of short diary entries as Pooter moves through life, suffering sometimes, other times having a grand old time as he tells off ironmongers, cabmen, friends, the lady who does his shirts, his son, co-workers and so on. Though I thought the book a little strained and where others claim to have 'laughed out loud,' I just sort of smiled, it does give an honest glimpse into the life of an 'ordinary joe' in 1890's England. I had to look up some words, which perhaps I should have known, like, what's a scraper? And when Lupin buys a 'pony-trap,' what's that? Pooter's wife is always shopping, too, or sending the 'girl' to get a fresh leg of mutton or an 'extra chop.' Because yeah, well there's really no refrigeration, not the kind one can depend on, now is there? They're also excited to be able to afford an extra 'chimney glass,' which I also looked up, so as far as seeing into the day-to-day life of a middle-class British family, circa 1892, the book is a small gem.<br /><br />The premise also reminded me of the British TV show, 'Keeping Up Appearances,' because though Pooter is always reminding himself, and his son, how an ordinary life with ordinary aspirations can bring lasting happiness, he also makes every effort to 'improve his station,' if only he can. <br /><br />Written by two comic writers from the same era as Charlie, this must have been a real hoot at one time. Now I can only smile, yeah, that's funny, sort of ...<br /><br />Three stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="17988747852966660418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_311598765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311598765" />
    <a title="Jade Heslin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8738313-jade-heslin"><img alt="Jade Heslin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393335002p2/8738313.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311598765?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jade Heslin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jade Heslin" href="/user/show/8738313-jade-heslin">Jade Heslin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311598765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18237516156426655074">This book had me in stitches. Mixing the hilarity of puns (the BEST form of comedy) with the humorously uneventful diary entries of a Victorian Gentleman makes for terrific reading. Mr. Pooter is undoubtedly one of the best comic characters I have ever had the pleasure of reading about. His failed attempts at witticisms filled me with joy, for at least I found him funny, if no one else did.<br/>How can you not love this man?:<br/><br/>“He said he wouldn’t stay, as he didn’t care much for the smell of the paint</span>
  <span id="freeText18237516156426655074" style="display:none">This book had me in stitches. Mixing the hilarity of puns (the BEST form of comedy) with the humorously uneventful diary entries of a Victorian Gentleman makes for terrific reading. Mr. Pooter is undoubtedly one of the best comic characters I have ever had the pleasure of reading about. His failed attempts at witticisms filled me with joy, for at least I found him funny, if no one else did.<br />How can you not love this man?:<br /><br />“He said he wouldn’t stay, as he didn’t care much for the smell of the paint, and fell over the scraper as he went out. Must get the scraper removed, or else I shall get into a scrape. I don’t often make jokes.”<br /><br />“You’re not going to complain of the smell of paint again?” He said: “No, not this time; but I’ll tell you what, I distinctly smell dry rot.” I don’t often make jokes, but I replied: “You’re talking a lot of dry rot yourself.” I could not help roaring at this, and Carrie said her sides quite ached with laughter. I never was so immensely tickled by anything I have ever said before. I actually woke up twice during the night, and laughed till the bed shook.”<br /><br />Carrie brought down some of my shirts and advised me to take them to Trillip’s round the corner. She said: “The fronts and cuffs are much frayed.” I said without a moment’s hesitation: “I’m ’frayed they are.” Lor! how we roared. I thought we should never stop laughing. As I happened to be sitting next the driver going to town on the ’bus, I told him my joke about the “frayed” shirts. I thought he would have rolled off his seat. They laughed at the office a good bit too over it.<br /><br />Mr Pooter = You LEGEND.<br />I only gave this 4 stars because the story was lacking, but I guess that’s what you should expect from a book entitled “Diary of a Nobody”.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18237516156426655074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133799945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12651680706110035943">Funny enough to keep me reading it to the end, but not funny enough to make me do anything else except to occasionally smile to myself.<br/><br/>Originally published on instalments in a magazine from May, 1888 to May 1889 this is the diary of the fictional Charles Pooter who justified keeping and publishing it in an Introductions where he said:<br/><br/>"Why should I not publish my diary? I have often seen reminiscences of people I have never even heard of, and I fail to see--because I do not happen to be a 'Somebo</span>
  <span id="freeText12651680706110035943" style="display:none">Funny enough to keep me reading it to the end, but not funny enough to make me do anything else except to occasionally smile to myself.<br /><br />Originally published on instalments in a magazine from May, 1888 to May 1889 this is the diary of the fictional Charles Pooter who justified keeping and publishing it in an Introductions where he said:<br /><br />"Why should I not publish my diary? I have often seen reminiscences of people I have never even heard of, and I fail to see--because I do not happen to be a 'Somebody'--why my diary should not be interesting. My only regret is that I did not commence it when I was a youth."<br /><br />The humor here is situational, slapstick and word plays. Its British flavor is strong, often Greek to me as I am more partial to Asian or Latin American humorists. Better than "Lucky Jim" (Martin Amis) and "Under the Net" (Iris Murdoch) but definitely inferior to "Gabriela, Clove and Cinnamon" (Jorge Amado) and even to a fellow British "Thank You, Jeeves" (P.G. Wodehouse). I don't care if this book is a British cultural icon. I like what I like!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12651680706110035943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_189892284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189892284" />
    <a title="Tenzin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5884561-tenzin"><img alt="Tenzin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499485988p2/5884561.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189892284?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tenzin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tenzin" href="/user/show/5884561-tenzin">Tenzin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189892284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8254747585229997210">The Diary of a Nobody is so unobtrusive, modest, and natural a piece of work that missing it completely could be forgiven (well, almost). It is a thoroughly obscure piece of writing armed with a unique format that provides for riveting and instinctive comedy — which cannot but make this seem a very peculiar achievement; a masterpiece nonetheless, albeit a seemingly accidental one. This peculiar achievement is the diary of a Charles Pooter, a nobody to himself and others, who asserts his right to</span>
  <span id="freeText8254747585229997210" style="display:none">The Diary of a Nobody is so unobtrusive, modest, and natural a piece of work that missing it completely could be forgiven (well, almost). It is a thoroughly obscure piece of writing armed with a unique format that provides for riveting and instinctive comedy — which cannot but make this seem a very peculiar achievement; a masterpiece nonetheless, albeit a seemingly accidental one. This peculiar achievement is the diary of a Charles Pooter, a nobody to himself and others, who asserts his right to record events in spite of it. I explored his 'I had teas' and 'Sent my dress-coats' with rapt attention, experiencing it, never mocking it. This social failure, patriarchal failure, is so unintentionally comic in his manners and his daily existence that all I did was laugh as is human and find kindred spiritedness as is also human. I grinned at Pooter's self-important absurdity along with his friends and knavish son, Lupin, but in a manner suggesting that Pooter and his unconscious gaffes are refreshing and even lovable. The writers do not patronise this hapless clerk; there is no hint of facile aspirations as there is no invitation to laugh at social gaffes, the response elicited is very much dependent on the reader more than anything else. This lack of snobbery is refreshing, it's earnestness shining through. The book itself is pleasant and undemanding. Pooter's errors are manifestations of our own, his idiosyncracies relatable and his profound anguish on being thought of as a complete and utter dunce - mirror-like!, confirming that we are all nobodys (bringing to trial if there is indeed a somebody) thus, making this book an ironical self-indulgence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8254747585229997210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3146178268238458895">READ ME <b>READ ME</b> READ ME<br/><br/><b>
  <u>May 16</u>
</b><br/>I'm floored - positively flabbergasted - that I've never heard of this book until now. It took my looking for something to read from my list of <i>1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die.</i>This is the hysterically funny account of Mr. Pooter, a typical, suburban, middle-class Englishman written in the late 1800s. And yet, it rarely read like a "classic" - it felt contemporary.<br/><br/><b>

  <span id="freeText3146178268238458895" style="display:none">READ ME <b>READ ME</b> READ ME<br /><br /><b>
  <u>May 16</u>
</b><br />I'm floored - positively flabbergasted - that I've never heard of this book until now. It took my looking for something to read from my list of <i>1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die.</i>This is the hysterically funny account of Mr. Pooter, a typical, suburban, middle-class Englishman written in the late 1800s. And yet, it rarely read like a "classic" - it felt contemporary.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>May 17</u>
</b><br />It reminded me a lot of a movie called <i>The Money Pit</i> with Tom Hanks in the beginning as Mr. Pooter shared his experiences and pitfalls with common home improvement projects. And, all throughout the book, I envisioned the narrator's voice as that of a stuffy, English person - making it that much more enjoyable. All throughout the book, Pooter attempts to be funny making puns, and while they are occasionally cute, the best part is Pooter's reaction to these puns, laughing outrageously at himself and thinking himself so clever. He's a riot!<br /><br />It's a fairly quick read, and it's not heavy at all. Just the normal everyday life of a normal everyday man trying to get a leg up in this world. I would highly recommend this book to anyone looking for a recommendation for one of the books on THE LIST.</span>

<div id="review_3579222462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3579222462" />
    <a title="Tom Britz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/680130-tom-britz"><img alt="Tom Britz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329910783p2/680130.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3579222462?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Britz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Britz" href="/user/show/680130-tom-britz">Tom Britz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781509881390" href="/book/show/39863336-the-diary-of-a-nobody">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3579222462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12934865654307739848">The Diary of A Nobody reads like a collection of old Vaudville skits. It is a humorous collections of diary entries by George Pooter, who is as plain as milk, but has a much higher opinion of himself and his station in life. His wife, Carrie is a long suffering, yet loving partner. His son William Pooter, is a pain in the neck whose first words upon his introduction back home, is to tell his father that he's no longer to be called William, but by his middle name, Lupin. I found this to be the mo</span>
  <span id="freeText12934865654307739848" style="display:none">The Diary of A Nobody reads like a collection of old Vaudville skits. It is a humorous collections of diary entries by George Pooter, who is as plain as milk, but has a much higher opinion of himself and his station in life. His wife, Carrie is a long suffering, yet loving partner. His son William Pooter, is a pain in the neck whose first words upon his introduction back home, is to tell his father that he's no longer to be called William, but by his middle name, Lupin. I found this to be the most amusing part of the book, imagining wanting to be called, Lupin Pooter.<br />The diary is a rambling exposure of a dull, suburban Victorian existence. George and Carrie are invited to a lot of household parties where something always goes wrong. I found this short novel to be humorous in a quiet sort of way. The jokes that George and the others find knee slapping hilarious are basically plays on words. One of Georges most amusing jokes was his telling his tailor that he's 'fraid that his cuffs are frayed. It took me a while to settle back down after that one. <br /> It is a quick read, but it's not a novel where the characters grow or change. It's a dull person's diary of mediocrity. The fun is that George considers his life to be, in his mind anyway, something to be looked up to. However dull the humor seems to today's audience, the book has never been out of print in 132 years. That's some staying power.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12934865654307739848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3579222462">
